          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: $('box').getStyle('z-index')})});
        } else {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});
        }
      });

      newTip['wrapper'].addClassName('prototipAllowOverflow');

        $('edition_1029002').observe('prototip:shown', function () {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {
            if ($('edition_1029002').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });
      $('edition_1029002').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]&gt;
</script></div>
</li>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_328066">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/328066" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/31973-claire" class="left imgcol" title="Claire"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1426315433p2/31973.jpg" alt="Claire" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/328066?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 27, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/31973-claire" class="user" title="Claire">Claire</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/31973-claire?shelf=books-with-swordfighting-in-them" class="actionLinkLite">books-with-swordfighting-in-them</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/31973-claire?shelf=favorite-books-ever" class="actionLinkLite">favorite-books-ever</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/31973-claire?shelf=books-i-loved-in-high-school" class="actionLinkLite">books-i-loved-in-high-school</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/31973-claire?shelf=five-stars" class="actionLinkLite">five-stars</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/31973-claire?shelf=fantasy-sci-fi-horror-geekery" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy-sci-fi-horror-geekery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer328066">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3127079510968467869">In 2007 I joined Goodreads and wrote reviews of some of the books that had most transformed me as a reader. I have since, over the years, taken an absurd amount of geek pride that my review of this book is (I think) the most popular one. And for everyone writing "GET OVER YOURSELF" in the comments, as a response to my using my own little corner of the internet to tell a story about how my life as a writer and a Catholic and a woman was shaped by this book, there were a dozen other women respondi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3127079510968467869">In 2007 I joined Goodreads and wrote reviews of some of the books that had most transformed me as a reader. I have since, over the years, taken an absurd amount of geek pride that my review of this book is (I think) the most popular one. And for everyone writing "GET OVER YOURSELF" in the comments, as a response to my using my own little corner of the internet to tell a story about how my life as a writer and a Catholic and a woman was shaped by this book, there were a dozen other women responding "OH MY GOD, ARE YOU ME?" I love that. I love this weird little internet mini-community we've built out of being weirdo outcast girls who felt inspired and empowered by this book about a weirdo outcast girl who becomes a raging badass. <br /><br />And then today I read this: <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.teleread.com/writing/marion-zimmer-bradley-child-abuser-says-daughter/" target="_blank">http://www.teleread.com/writing/mario...</a><br /><br />And this: <a rel="nofollow" href="http://deirdre.net/marion-zimmer-bradley-gave-us-new-perspectives-all-right/" target="_blank">http://deirdre.net/marion-zimmer-brad...</a><br /><br />And this: <a rel="nofollow" href="http://deirdre.net/marion-zimmer-bradley-its-worse-than-i-knew/" target="_blank">http://deirdre.net/marion-zimmer-brad...</a><br /><br />And this: <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.theguardian.com/books/2014/jun/27/sff-community-marion-zimmer-bradley-daughter-accuses-abuse" target="_blank">http://www.theguardian.com/books/2014...</a><br /><br />And about twenty more.<br /><br />Every consumer of art gets to decide for themselves how much the life of the artist matters to them. Sometimes brilliant creative geniuses are assholes. Sometimes they're criminals. Sometimes that makes a difference to how you read their work. Sometimes it doesn't. <br /><br />The words of twenty-six-year-old me, pouring forth my passionate love for MZB's words, remain untouched and unedited below. Because that story, of how I fell in love with that book as a child, is still a true story. I haven't decided whether I will re-read this book again, whether I will keep it or get rid of it, knowing the things I know now about the woman who wrote it. And I'm not telling you what you should do. <br /><br />But MZB's daughter says out loud not only that her mother abused her, but that part of the reason she hid that abuse was because of MZB's status in the SFF community as a champion of women. Because she didn't think anyone would believe her. Because this is an important feminist work. Because her mother's fans would be angry at her for accusing their icon of such horrors. <br /><br />And I won't be complicit in that. <br /><br />--Claire Willett (June 27, 2014)<br /><br /><br />ORIGINAL REVIEW BELOW<br /><br /><br />________________________________________<br /><br /><br />You have to be a particular kind of girl to fall in love with this book the way I did.<br /><br />--You have to be in the sixth grade, a freakishly precocious reader, whose beloved sixth-grade teacher brings a box of her ten favorite books to class and sets them up on the chalkboard and leaves them there for weeks for you to look at, including one HUGE book that looks like it's a billion pages long with some cool fairy priestess chick on a horse on the cover.<br /><br />--You have to have grown up reading King Arthur stories and LOVE the movie "The Sword In the Stone."<br /><br />--You have to be so hopelessly nerdy that you would rather sit on the side of the playground reading than play kickball, never mind how much the other kids make fun of you about it.<br /><br />--You have to be Catholic enough to understand the mentality of the occasionally hateful Christian characters in the book (as well as to be baffled and perplexed by all the sexuality which will make a number of plot elements only make sense to you when you re-read the book as a college student and go, "Ohhhhh. Now I get it").<br /><br />--You have to be the kind of girl who loves and relates to the plain outcast Morgaine who is treated as a freak has to learn how to rely on herself alone. <br /><br />--You have to hate the shallow blonde princesses, even when they seem like they might be kind of nice people, and always root for the feisty brunette. <br /><br />--You have to be a fantasy geek who LOVES any book with swordfighting, magic, princesses, and doomed romance. <br /><br />--You have to be patient enough to read 800+ pages that cover one woman's entire lifetime from before her birth to old, old age.<br /><br />--You have to come to the end of the book and secretly wish that (despite your religious conviction in your Catholic upbringing) Britain had never been Christianized and we were all still witches. <br /><br />--You have to secretly wish you belonged to a mystical female cult where you had to have a blue crescent moon tattooed on your forehead.<br /><br />--You have to wish you knew how to ride a horse in a dress and look majestic, instead of falling off every time you were forced onto a horse at camp or on vacation and now you hate them and they scare you. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3127079510968467869">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_27792932">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/27792932" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1083872-rachel" class="left imgcol" title="Rachel"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1208222543p2/1083872.jpg" alt="Rachel" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/27792932?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 20, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1083872-rachel" class="user" title="Rachel">Rachel</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1083872-rachel?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer27792932">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17025024641635001853">OK I admit, when I told my college Arthurian Lit professor that I'd read and enjoyed this book, he proceeded to give me a quick-before-the-next-class-comes-in lecture about how Marion Zimmer Bradley's "interpretation" skewed wildly from the genre.<br /><br />But I don't care. It's a difficult book (long and utterly depressing,) but it takes the first in-depth look at both women and the pagan Celtic religion of Britain, which Christianity usurped around that time. Evil sorceress Morgan Le Fay is transfered i</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17025024641635001853">OK I admit, when I told my college Arthurian Lit professor that I'd read and enjoyed this book, he proceeded to give me a quick-before-the-next-class-comes-in lecture about how Marion Zimmer Bradley's "interpretation" skewed wildly from the genre.<br /><br />But I don't care. It's a difficult book (long and utterly depressing,) but it takes the first in-depth look at both women and the pagan Celtic religion of Britain, which Christianity usurped around that time. Evil sorceress Morgan Le Fay is transfered into multi-faceted Morgaine, a woman deeply committed to her family, especially her aunt, Viviane, half-brother, Arthur, and cousin, Lancelet. Gwenhyfar, the simpering Christian princess, was my least favorite, but even she had some complexity, an unhappy childhood, inferiority complex made worse by her bareness, (and obvious jealousy-issues with Morgaine concerning the womens' relationships with Arthur and Lancelet.) Perhaps the most dour of Morgaine's familial ties is that with her son, Mordred, the illigitimate heir of her brother, whom she foolishly put up with her aunt Morgause, easily the most shallow and greedy woman in the entire book.<br /><br />Religion-wise, I found it impossible not to root for Morgaine's Avalon, not only because I knew it was destined to recede into the mists, but because it was matriarchal, and so much more comforting to me than the expansionalist, narrow-minded and mysoginistic version of Christianity prevalent during those times. At the end, Morgaine herslf shows the most tolerance and versatility for diverse cultures- she outlives most everyone else, and grows to accept that her mother goddess is now worshipped as the virgin mary. Quite the contrast from the crone-like Morgan Le Fay, whose only purpose is to destroy the kingdom of Camelot.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17025024641635001853">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14966495" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/649064-matthew" class="left imgcol" title="Matthew"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1202671445p2/649064.jpg" alt="Matthew" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/14966495?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 09, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/649064-matthew" class="user" title="Matthew">Matthew</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/649064-matthew?shelf=fantastic" class="actionLinkLite">fantastic</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/649064-matthew?shelf=books-on-pod" class="actionLinkLite">books-on-pod</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer14966495">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4956007884867203104">The Arthur myth from the point of view of Morgaine le Fay, pagan priestess. Supposedly a feminist take on the old legends. There is one main problem with this approach: let's face it, women's lives in the dark ages were pretty boring. And rather than break out of this mold with strong female characters, Bradley talks a lot about spinning, weaving, and having babies. The female characters are either contemptible or irritating, or both. The male characters are cardboard--Arthur is as heroic as a l</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4956007884867203104">The Arthur myth from the point of view of Morgaine le Fay, pagan priestess. Supposedly a feminist take on the old legends. There is one main problem with this approach: let's face it, women's lives in the dark ages were pretty boring. And rather than break out of this mold with strong female characters, Bradley talks a lot about spinning, weaving, and having babies. The female characters are either contemptible or irritating, or both. The male characters are cardboard--Arthur is as heroic as a limp dishrag, Merlin just an old man sitting in his rocking chair. The pagan-Christian thing is overwrought and shrill, devolving mostly into interminable theological debates between characters that cover the same ground over and over and over again.<br /><br />A lot of things irritated me about this book, but nothing more than the simple lack of a compelling narrative construction. Nothing happens. There is dialogue, which mostly rehashes things that were already talked about. And then there is monologue, in which the weak and mostly contemptible characters thrash around in their heads so much that it would make Dostoyevsky cringe. It makes me angry that you could even try to tell the Arthurian legend--even from a feminine point of view--without looking at the epic clashes between the knights of the round table and their enemies.<br /><br />Horrendously disappointing. For a fantasy novel, George R.R. Martin's <a title="A Game of Thrones (A Song of Ice and Fire, #1) by George R.R. Martin" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/13496.A_Game_of_Thrones__A_Song_of_Ice_and_Fire___1_">A Game of Thrones</a> does everything that this book tries to do, and does it ten times better. For more of a historical view, Bernard Cornwell's <a title="The Last Kingdom (The Saxon Stories, #1) by Bernard Cornwell" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/68527.The_Last_Kingdom__The_Saxon_Stories___1_">The Last Kingdom</a> is a much more fun and interesting look at the clash of Christian and pagan civilizations, and even has characters that you don't hate. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4956007884867203104">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1119984240779883650">The fact is that this is one of the most popular books in the world and has a cult following. It is not uncommon for fans to have read it 10, 20, 30 times. Thousands of people from everywhere make pilgrimages to Glastonbury because of it. This is because there are deep truths in it that move people tremendously. Although the writing is not perfect (it's far better in the prequel, LADY OF AVALON,) the story itself and some key sections are simply stunning! Zimmer's reworking of the legend from the viewpoint of the women in power is brilliant and highly successful in the literary world. It is sacred to me and millions of other women.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_180340370">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/180340370" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1203920-virag" class="left imgcol" title="Virag"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Virag" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/180340370?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 29, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1203920-virag" class="user" title="Virag">Virag</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer180340370">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15052792568137081468">Good lord, I haven't ever hated a book as much as this one.<br /><br />I picked up <em>The Mists of Avalon</em> because I really love Nordic myths, and usually any stories about King Arthur. Everyone seems to adore this book; even my librarian told me that this was a really good Arthurian tale! Well, it's not. It's horrible.<br /><br />First, let me say how turned off I was by all the bashing and hating there was of Christianity. And I'm saying this as the atheist that I am-- I don't believe in God, yet that doesn't mean I am n</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15052792568137081468">Good lord, I haven't ever hated a book as much as this one.<br /><br />I picked up <em>The Mists of Avalon</em> because I really love Nordic myths, and usually any stories about King Arthur. Everyone seems to adore this book; even my librarian told me that this was a really good Arthurian tale! Well, it's not. It's horrible.<br /><br />First, let me say how turned off I was by all the bashing and hating there was of Christianity. And I'm saying this as the atheist that I am-- I don't believe in God, yet that doesn't mean I am not bothered by the unnecessary, and often narrow-minded and uneducated hate towards a religion. That's all there was in the first 150 pages, and it was a very recurring theme throughout the book. As if having one stupid priest wasn't enough, the authur just had to fit in several more and call each stupider than the previous.<br />Yeah, there were dumb/evil priests and followers of that religion, but there will always be bad people who call themselves followers of a religion. However, that doesn't mean the religion is outright evil, okay? There were dumber than sh!t Christians, Jews, Muslims, atheists and whatnot and there always will be. But you can't call a whole sect bad based on it's worst people. If you did that then you might as well hate the entire human race and live in a hole the rest of your life because there are some really evil/idiotic people out there. <br /><br />And all this blabbering about stupid, mean, cowardly priests did nothing to advance the plot. At all. I mean if you wanted to write a book in which the antagonists were all evil Christians then you're on the right track, but this was supposed to be a book on King Arthur, dang it, not of your personal hate issues with Christians. Personally, I really don't like it when authors bring up religions in books that aren't nonfiction theology/psychology/history works. Stuff gets brushed over and embellished to make the book in question seem more interesting; and it's infuriating what you'll read. That's exactly the case with <em>The Mists of Avalon</em>. Keep that in mind, Marion Zimmer Bradley. You can rant to a therapist, but don't take it out on a book <s>THAT IS SUPPOSED TO BE ABOUT A WELL-LOVED FANTASY TALE.</s> Just don't take it out on a book in general. <br />Although, based on this book's rating, people loved all the hate on Christians. *Snort* People these days shovel down any garbage given to them.<br /><br />What's worse, the author couldn't seem to create a halfway decent female protagonist in all the 800+ pages and the countless women characters.<br /><br />Igraine? No. She's a whiney pushover who convinces herself she's in love with a man her husband hates because she hears a prophecy that she's supposed to bear that man's child (if I heard a prophecy like that, I'd run away screaming, avoid the guy who's supposed to knock me up, and in case he did come near me I'd at least have pepper spray handy). Okay, she didn't love Gorlois, but he was good to her up until she started spending time with a man he clearly distrusted and told her to stay away from (not that I really like Gorlois, but wouldn't you be <em>freaking mad</em> if your wife/husband got all cozy with someone you consider evil?). And besides stabbing her husband, Igraine doesn't do anything. She just sits at home. Wow, my new hero.<br /><br />Vivianne plotted adultery and incest, acted like she loved everyone, but in reality, did horrible things to them. She was even surprised when Galahad, who she barely had anything to do with, didn't love her. Also, did anyone else notice how Vivianne always corrected people and told them "All gods and goddesses are one" and then proceeded to ridicule the Christian God and call him and his followers morons? Hah. I don't know if that was meant to be funny, or if Ms. Bradely was just too stupid to notice the contradiction. <br /><br />Morgaine failed at everything in life. I felt bad for her at being used like that, then for being rejected by Lancelet. But then again, she couldn't care less for her child, hated pretty much innocent Gwenhwyfar, and came up with plans to have Lancelet sleep with her even though she knew he didn't love her; so whatever sympathy I had for her went pretty quick. And it gets even better - she gets married, sleeps with several more people, goes to Avalon again and acts like she's the main goddess, even though she did so many un-goddess like things. I loved how Niniane thought "She should be here in my place, the GREAT Morgaine of the Fairies!" HAH! What did Morgaine ever do besides sleep with her own brother? <br /><br />Gwenhwyfar was worse than Igraine. On the other hand, it was so obvious that Bradely created her solely to make fun of Christians. She must have been like "Oh, I'll create this woman who's a dumb little bitch in heat who everyone will hate. And, bonus: I'll mention how beautiful she is every two pages to make sure all the female readers will be jealous of her and hate her even more! Then I'll make sure to have her pretend to be a pious follower of Christ (even though, in reality, an adulteress is <em>not</em> a pious follower of Christ) and I'll have created a perfect epitome of all Christian women to show the world what nasty morons they are! Yay!!"<br /><br />I mean really? And a lot of people actually agree with this exaggerated, biased, ridiculous <em>nonsense</em>.<br />When I was browsing through 5-star reviews of this book to try and understand why exactly human beings love this trash, someone actually said "...this book makes me want to leave behind my life and become a pagan..." ! (That wasn't the exact quote, but that was the gist of it.)<br />I just... I can't even... Oh yes I can. Become a pagan, then, and have a baby with your own brother at a drunken bonfire. Next, make sure you neglect your child and run around effing tons of other men! By the way, back then protection didn't exist; so be sure not to use any of that either, and see how many STDs and unwanted pregancies you'll get. Homeless and vulgar, isn't Morgaine's portrayal of a pagan lifestyle just wonderful?! <br /><br />Back to the characters - the males were all one-dimensional and flat. They were all extremely handsome and extremely skilled knights and extremely horny. *Coughs* A little originality, please, Bradely? Maybe divert from your view that all men are chauvinist pigs?<br />The one character I kind of liked was Morgause - she's independent, seemed to have a good relationship with her children, and kept Morgaine's secret. But she was so slutty that I stopped rooting for her.<br /><br />There also seemed to be a whole lot of describing boring day-to-day activities that, just like all the Christian hate, did nothing to advance the plot. It's like, somebody gets up, stares in the mirror and thinks about some complicated love web, goes downstairs, starts knitting, talks to an old woman who came in from the cold... *47 pages later something tiny happens that helps the story along*. <br />Then another good 1/5 of the book was made up of describing how beautiful this and this person was, and then another person is introduced who is WAY MORE beautiful, and so on and so on. In Morgaine's case, it was terrible. One minute she's plain, then someone calls her beautiful, then she's called ugly, then she's supposed to have an inner beauty, then Morgain does something horrible so I can't see what inner beauty they're talking about, then she's beautiful, ugly, beautiful, ugly...<br /><br />Like, did the author have bipolar attacks while writing this? I honestly don't spend a whole lot of time detailing characters' faces. Tell me they're tall, red-haired, have a scar on their face and that's just perfect. I'm interested in the plot, not the size of everybodys' big toes. Seriously!<br /><br />The writing itself could have been okay, but because its subject was crap, it was not okay. This book was bad, it really was. After a good 150 pages, I just skimmed another 500, and then skipped the last part and skimmed the epilogue. I honestly could care less what happens. <br /><br />I'm against book burning, but I'd probably spray extra kerosene on a stack of <em>The Mists of Avalon</em> copies if I had the chance.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15052792568137081468">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_792524427">

  <a href="/user/show/26681101-markus" class="left imgcol" title="Markus"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1421952301p2/26681101.jpg" alt="Markus" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/792524427?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 06, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/26681101-markus" class="user" title="Markus">Markus</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/26681101-markus?shelf=favourites" class="actionLinkLite">favourites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/26681101-markus?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/26681101-markus?shelf=to-reread" class="actionLinkLite">to-reread</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/26681101-markus?shelf=it-changed-my-life" class="actionLinkLite">it-changed-my-life</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/26681101-markus?shelf=arthurian-legend" class="actionLinkLite">arthurian-legend</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer792524427">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9976975924705029912"><em>"There is no such thing as a true tale. Truth has many faces and the truth is like to the old road to Avalon; it depends on your own will, and your own thoughts, whither the road will take you."</em><br /><br />Again, I feel the need to put my thoughts down about some of the books that changed my life and made me into the guy I am.<br /><br />Those who know me just one tiny bit also know that <em>The Lord of the Rings</em> is my favourite book ever. Go a little bit deeper, and you also know that Frank Herbert's <em>Dune</em> is high up on my</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9976975924705029912"><em>"There is no such thing as a true tale. Truth has many faces and the truth is like to the old road to Avalon; it depends on your own will, and your own thoughts, whither the road will take you."</em><br /><br />Again, I feel the need to put my thoughts down about some of the books that changed my life and made me into the guy I am.<br /><br />Those who know me just one tiny bit also know that <em>The Lord of the Rings</em> is my favourite book ever. Go a little bit deeper, and you also know that Frank Herbert's <em>Dune</em> is high up on my list of all-time favourites. The point is that those two books were the first real fantasy books (using the term loosely here) I read in my life. But... in reality I have a holy trinity of fantasy books from my childhood. The three books that made me love fantasy in the first place and go to explore other worlds and the magic between the pages of masterpieces.<br /><br />I've already confessed my undying love for <em>LotR</em> and <em>Dune</em>. The third aspect of the trinity is <em>The Mists of Avalon</em> by Marion Zimmer Bradley.<br /><br />Like I already wrote about in slightly more detail in my review of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/793272536">
  <em>Darkover Landfall</em>
</a>, Marion Zimmer Bradley is, despite it all, one of my favourite authors. This book is what introduced me to her works.<br /><br />But as I write these words, I realise one sad fact about this wonderful story. I remember nothing of it. Not one bit. Unlike the other two books mentioned in the beginning, I have never read this one again after the first time. And now I'm scared to actually do it. Because I don't want to sully childhood memories with the harshness of reality.<br /><br />The only thing I do remember is that I absolutely loved it. And still do, passionately so. And that's the important part, right?<br /><br />So, depending on whether or not I eventually read this again, and depending on whether or not I have more thoughts to think and more words to write, maybe there will be a full review about the loveliest work of Arthurian fiction at some point.<br /><br /><em>"And so, perhaps, the truth winds somewhere between the road to Glastonbury, Isle of the Priests, and the road to Avalon, lost forever in the mists of the Summer Sea."</em><br /><br /><img width="" height="" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1446818966i/16906648._SX540_.jpg" /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer122154830567037799">This is one of the few books that I hate. I'm a feminist and I love King Arthur stories and <em>The Mists of Avalon</em> makes me vaguely nauseous. I read the whole thing hoping it would get better, and it didn't, though there are a few good bits. Overall I found it offensive to the Arthurian legends, to history, and to women, and being a 15-year-old girl who liked fantasy novels did nothing to change this opinion.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_36308757">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/36308757" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1660443-jackie" class="left imgcol" title="Jackie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1225124705p2/1660443.jpg" alt="Jackie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/36308757?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 07, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1660443-jackie" class="user" title="Jackie">Jackie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1660443-jackie?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1660443-jackie?shelf=celtic" class="actionLinkLite">celtic</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1660443-jackie?shelf=arthurian" class="actionLinkLite">arthurian</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1660443-jackie?shelf=epic" class="actionLinkLite">epic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer36308757">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3249573966307269476">An excellent Arthurian saga.<br />Written from the point of view of Morgaine, Arthur's half-sister and the villian of traditional Arthur tales.<br />Unique in perspective with strong female characters. It is a story of love; and quite different from any Arthur novel you'll ever read.<br />Marion Zimmer Bradley's best work. She paints a vivid picture, rich with depth of characters and relationships. <br />One of my favorites, I can read this over and over again.<br /><br /><br /></span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_4873961">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4873961" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely" class="left imgcol" title="J.G. Keely"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1274333636p2/84023.jpg" alt="J.G. Keely" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/4873961?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 31, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely" class="user" title="J.G. Keely">J.G. Keely</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=contemporary-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">contemporary-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=novel" class="actionLinkLite">novel</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=america" class="actionLinkLite">america</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer4873961">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4659818933716308356">Though I am wont to blame the inescapability of genetics for various aspects of an Epicurean reading of Absurdism, I tend to pause, for some reason, in ascribing gender differences as stringently. It's difficult to say if this is simply a bias of wishful egalitarian thinking or truly an outgrowth of my understanding, for precisely the reasons that Epicureus is worthy to interrupt my many Suicides. So, when I say that women seem more than men to be capable of breaking the Tolkien Curse laid so th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4659818933716308356">Though I am wont to blame the inescapability of genetics for various aspects of an Epicurean reading of Absurdism, I tend to pause, for some reason, in ascribing gender differences as stringently. It's difficult to say if this is simply a bias of wishful egalitarian thinking or truly an outgrowth of my understanding, for precisely the reasons that Epicureus is worthy to interrupt my many Suicides. So, when I say that women seem more than men to be capable of breaking the Tolkien Curse laid so thickly upon Modern Fantasy (barely proper), it is with trepidation.<br /><br />Flatly blaming rude and wretched socialization always seems easier; despite our inability to understand any First Cause. Original Sin infects us all.<br /><br />There is certainly something bound in the flesh which drives a breed of dwarfish, ill-socialized, fetish-loving escapists to blindly build and habitate an unoriginal world; and for a further gaggle of the nearly less-talented to consume it ravenously. It seems that, in the spirit of contrariness, when women find themselves thrust by love of horses or exceedingly lax tonsorial concerns into the same arena, that they fight a different fight.<br /><br />Perhaps they approach the incline from a different vantage; arriving not by way of a)Tolkien to b)Conan to c)some unspeakable modern half-wit, but by Malory, McKinley, and Spenser. Of course, one must not forget that the vein of Fantasy still runs, at least in part, through Austen; and that though those alloys be rarer, still inhabit the edges.<br /><br />Bradley has certainly taken a different tack on her way to the summit (never tor) of fantasy. She evokes Spenser, the Idylls, and all manner of other ridiculous romanticics of the Arthurian Mythos. She also endeavors to pull the characters out of the romantic and toward post-modern psychological conflict. On occasion, she even succeeds.<br /><br />There is an undeniable depth to the books, accompanied by a rather pleasing graying at the temples of morality which immediately places her at the opposite pole from her male contemporaries. That those poles are really not so far away somewhat lessens the impact, and one is eventually bound to recognize that there really is a reverse pole to the whole of our concept of fantasy marked somewhere in Peake's Titus trilogy. <br /><br />Actually, that's not true. One could very easily read a fantasy novel a week for life and never have to realize that Bradley is really only a little bit out there; but certainly enough to feel like a breath of the fresher.<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://starsbeetlesandfools.blogspot.com/2012/06/suggested-readings-in-fantasy.html">My Fantasy Book Suggestions</a></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4659818933716308356">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer427768012068563065">Well, there I go again - sniffling and crying through the last 10 pages over a bunch of fictional characters that I feel I know better then some real people. If ever there was a book to make me believe in the power of magic, then Bradley cast her spell over me when she penned this book.<br /><br />What a sap I am, and what a sap I'll be again the next time I read this...<br /><br />:D<br /><br /></span>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fantasy lovers, arthurian scholars, young women, feminists</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1410402">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15773961100767529024">I read this book when I was in my mid-teens, and in the midst of an Arthurian obsession phase. These are mythical characters that have been written on so many times and by legendary figures who are almost myths themselves. It's a really hard subject to tackle without derision. I do think she filled a niche in what could otherwise be a very chauvinistic, idealized genre. <br /><br />I haven't read this recently, so I don't know if I would still connect to it as much as I did when I read it all those years ag</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15773961100767529024">I read this book when I was in my mid-teens, and in the midst of an Arthurian obsession phase. These are mythical characters that have been written on so many times and by legendary figures who are almost myths themselves. It's a really hard subject to tackle without derision. I do think she filled a niche in what could otherwise be a very chauvinistic, idealized genre. <br /><br />I haven't read this recently, so I don't know if I would still connect to it as much as I did when I read it all those years ago. It teaches something about never taking a story for granted, and the fact that there's a side even to the purportedly evil people that can be more sympathetic than we realize. It's like "Wicked" in that way, only less cliched. Plus, this one was first!</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_22510512">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/22510512" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1171340-nick" class="left imgcol" title="Nick"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" alt="Nick" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/22510512?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 25, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1171340-nick" class="user" title="Nick">Nick</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer22510512">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1756868415724879429">This book was awful. Oh my god, I had to put it down five times and read something else just to get through it. The terms pointless and self-serving come to mind as this author might as well beat the reader over the head with a piss poor attempt at Feminism and some kind of pot-shots at Christianity. I would consider myself a Feminist, but Feminism is about equality not role reversal. And I'm as agnostic as the next guy, but cut me some slack, all religions are just about the same. I mean, these</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1756868415724879429">This book was awful. Oh my god, I had to put it down five times and read something else just to get through it. The terms pointless and self-serving come to mind as this author might as well beat the reader over the head with a piss poor attempt at Feminism and some kind of pot-shots at Christianity. I would consider myself a Feminist, but Feminism is about equality not role reversal. And I'm as agnostic as the next guy, but cut me some slack, all religions are just about the same. I mean, these characters are weak, the story is weak, the "meaning" is lost in total bull. I dont know, maybe I missed the point being a guy, but that seems more like an excuse for the author. I couldnt connect or feel anything for any of the characters, from Arther and Lancelot to Morgan and Guinevere, all of the characters were just weak. The author spread herself too thinly.<br /><br />It may just be me, I'm a huge fan of the Arther legend, and I found the attempt to shift the focus to the woman behind the men fall short, I had hopes for the story and I just dont think this book did a good job. If you want a really amazing Arther Legend try Bernard Cornwell's series: <em> Winter King, Enemy of God, Excalibur. </em> It sticks a bit more to the classic "hero" Arther, but that series "does it" for me. I just didnt like this book, sorry, but I think she could have told this story in 50 pages instead of 800.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1756868415724879429">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_198563973">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/198563973" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3131288-rachael-sherwood" class="left imgcol" title="Rachael Sherwood"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1265741631p2/3131288.jpg" alt="Rachael Sherwood" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/198563973?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 21, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3131288-rachael-sherwood" class="user" title="Rachael Sherwood">Rachael Sherwood</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3131288-rachael-sherwood?shelf=2011" class="actionLinkLite">2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer198563973">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4800563904308819277">When I was about a fourth of the way through The Mists of Avalon, I glanced at some reviews on GoodReads and was disheartened to see that the consensus of many reviews was that the book ended on a FEMINISMRULESMENDROOLSCHRISTIANITYSUX message. Thus far I had found the book to be more complex than that, but I could see that ending coming, as MZB is not always the subtlest of writers. However, at the end I happily conclude that seeing such a reductionist message from the text is a failing on the r</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4800563904308819277">When I was about a fourth of the way through The Mists of Avalon, I glanced at some reviews on GoodReads and was disheartened to see that the consensus of many reviews was that the book ended on a FEMINISMRULESMENDROOLSCHRISTIANITYSUX message. Thus far I had found the book to be more complex than that, but I could see that ending coming, as MZB is not always the subtlest of writers. However, at the end I happily conclude that seeing such a reductionist message from the text is a failing on the reader, not the author. <br /><br />The Mists of Avalon, for those who are not familiar, is a retelling of the Arthurian saga from the women’s point of view. Most notably, it follows the women of Avalon, traditionally regarded as witches, crones, and villainesses. Morgaine (Morgan Le Fey) is not evil sorcercess intent on destroying the good Christian king, she is a devoted priestess to the Goddess who wants to make sure that her religion is not destroyed by the Christian conversion of the lands. The decline of the pagan religion is symbolized quite literally, through their holy Isle of Avalon. There was a time when any man or woman could find the Island, but as more and more converts abandon the old ways, Avalon fades more into the mists. Morgaine’s foil is Gwynhefar, the passionate young Queen who wants to remake Britain in Christ’s name. Both Morgaine and Gwynhefar are I think what you could fairly describe as religious fanatics, and they both struggle with what they must give up to push their agenda. The Merlin of Britain, leader of the Druids, occupies a middle ground in this religious debate, saying merely that all Gods are one and that it does not matter what form men see them in. In the end, the book’s conclusion lies closest to the Merlin’s, and even Morgaine comes to embrace the Christians and sees her Goddess in the Virgin Mary and Saint Brigid. I think this book is a great starting place for a bigger discussion of the place of feminine spirituality within a patriarchal driven religion. <br /><br />Although the book is largely concerned with matters of religion, it is also a saga of family and love and is filled with fascinating characters. It’s hard for me to say who I’d recommend it to, or not. For me it was a totally immersive and exciting experience, I suspect for others it would drag. I would NOT recommend it for someone looking for an Arthurian story, this is a postfeminist story about spirituality. If that interests you, go for it. ;) I think possibly it’s best audience would be teenage girls; I really enjoyed reading it as an adult, as a teen I think I would have just loved it.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4800563904308819277">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <a href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl" class="left imgcol" title="Tadiana ? Night Owl?"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1383845644p2/8734459.jpg" alt="Tadiana ? Night Owl?" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_14066038">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14066038" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/855228-maurean" class="left imgcol" title="Maurean"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1201869134p2/855228.jpg" alt="Maurean" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/14066038?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 20, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/855228-maurean" class="user" title="Maurean">Maurean</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer14066038">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17141028964641831772">I have heard for years nothing but glowing recommendations for this book, yet I am still amazed by the intensity with which this story touched me. Marion Zimmer Bradley is an incredible storyteller with impressive knowledge of the ancient Goddess based spirituality. The history and mysticism are clearly well-researched, and the writing is lyrical, palpable, and quite beautiful. <br /><br />In this “retelling” of the Arthurian legend- which parallels, too, the Celtic mythology of Finn MacCool &amp; the Fenia</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17141028964641831772">I have heard for years nothing but glowing recommendations for this book, yet I am still amazed by the intensity with which this story touched me. Marion Zimmer Bradley is an incredible storyteller with impressive knowledge of the ancient Goddess based spirituality. The history and mysticism are clearly well-researched, and the writing is lyrical, palpable, and quite beautiful. <br /><br />In this “retelling” of the Arthurian legend- which parallels, too, the Celtic mythology of Finn MacCool &amp; the Fenians, and the Red Branch Heroes of Ulster - from the perspective of the women in the story, it details magically the struggles of Christianity vs. Paganism, where the lines between good and evil are much blurred;<br />All the characters are well-drawn in depths both good and bad, flawed and noble, completely and ultimately human. <br /><br />My favorite passage:<br /><br />“<em>That faith </em>[Christianity]<em> seems too simple to me – the idea that we have only to believe that Christ died for our sins, once and for all. But I know too much of the truth…of the way life works, with life after life in which we, ourselves, and only we, can work out the causes we have set in motion and make amends for the harm we have done. It stands not in the realm of reason that one man, however holy and blessed, could atone for all of the sins of all men, done in all lifetimes.</em>” (pg. 611)<br /><br />This is a story of how the old pagan ways of Avalon are engulfed by the rise of Christianity after the fall of Rome. Portrayed through the story of Camelot, told in the voice of Morgaine, Morgan La Fey, niece of Viviane, Lady of the Lake; queen of Cornwall, sister to the High King, and consort to the King Stag; a refreshing perspective to a well-known tale. <br /><br />This is a masterful interpretation, giving new life to all the old characters: Gwenhwyfar, the pious, Christian Queen to Authur; The handsome Lancelet, most honorable knight and Queen’s Champion; Taliesin, the Great Merlin of Britian, Morgause, Morgaine’s Aunt and foster-mother to Galahad…truly, an engrossing and engaging tale of epic proportions; I cannot find words to praise it enough. This will procure a spot in my “favorite five fiction” reads of all time. If you have not yet treated yourself to this enchanting feat of imagination into this world of Old, I urge you to do so, soon. It is well worth the journey<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17141028964641831772">...more</a>

          </span>

  <a href="/user/show/602053-linda" class="left imgcol" title="Linda"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1194322002p2/602053.jpg" alt="Linda" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/8699132?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 05, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/602053-linda" class="user" title="Linda">Linda</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/602053-linda?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/602053-linda?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer8699132">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15865483262565856393">This book is one of those that I would consider required reading. Marion Zimmer Bradley's telling of the Arthurian legend from the point of view of Morgaine is so captivating that even twenty years later, I come back to it.<br /><br />It's the story of Britain after Rome has faded but the influence of Rome, particularly through spreading Christianity hasn't. Britain is on the cusp where the spread of Christianity is eclipsing the native, ancient religion. You'll see all the familiar names from the legend, A</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15865483262565856393">This book is one of those that I would consider required reading. Marion Zimmer Bradley's telling of the Arthurian legend from the point of view of Morgaine is so captivating that even twenty years later, I come back to it.<br /><br />It's the story of Britain after Rome has faded but the influence of Rome, particularly through spreading Christianity hasn't. Britain is on the cusp where the spread of Christianity is eclipsing the native, ancient religion. You'll see all the familiar names from the legend, Arthur, Guinevere, etc., but their roles may not be exactly what you expect. In Bradley's tale, Morgaine is a priestess of Avalon who tries to serve the Goddess, the Lady of Avalon, her King and brother and is ill-used in the process. For me the book succeeds because when I read it I got the sense that it could have happened like that (if you're willing to suspend disbelief enough for the magical elements of the story).<br /><br />When I first read this, I couldn't put it down. If you haven't read it, you should remedy that!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15865483262565856393">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_248194995">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/248194995" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4884555-rameau" class="left imgcol" title="rameau"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1341336616p2/4884555.jpg" alt="rameau" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/248194995?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 18, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4884555-rameau" class="user" title="rameau">rameau</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/84527.The_Mists_of_Avalon" title="Hardcover 9780345441188" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4884555-rameau?shelf=genre-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">genre-fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4884555-rameau?shelf=annoying-1st-3rd-voice-mixing" class="actionLinkLite">annoying-1st-3rd-voice-mixing</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4884555-rameau?shelf=read-in-2012" class="actionLinkLite">read-in-2012</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4884555-rameau?shelf=genre-arthurian-legends" class="actionLinkLite">genre-arthurian-legends</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/4884555-rameau?shelf=language-english-original" class="actionLinkLite">language-english-original</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer248194995">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1639863693668981006"><em>This review can also be found on <a rel="nofollow" href="http://books-forlife.blogspot.com/2012/10/rameau-reviews-mists-of-avalon-marion.html">Book Girl of Mur-y-Castell</a>-blog.</em><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />I’ve been actively reading and reviewing books for a year and a half now. In that time, my criteria for rating a book on the one to five stars scale has changed a couple of times. A few things still hold true. The book has to be exceptional and leave an indelible impression to get a five star rating from me. Three stars remains my meh-rating. It’s a book that I can objectively call a good one, something I might have even enjoyed re</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1639863693668981006"><em>This review can also be found on <a rel="nofollow" href="http://books-forlife.blogspot.com/2012/10/rameau-reviews-mists-of-avalon-marion.html">Book Girl of Mur-y-Castell</a>-blog.</em><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />I’ve been actively reading and reviewing books for a year and a half now. In that time, my criteria for rating a book on the one to five stars scale has changed a couple of times. A few things still hold true. The book has to be exceptional and leave an indelible impression to get a five star rating from me. Three stars remains my meh-rating. It’s a book that I can objectively call a good one, something I might have even enjoyed reading, but it’s also something I can easily forget and move on.<br /><br />My one star rating however, that’s changed the most. At first it was anything and everything I simply didn’t like. If the offences added up to a certain point I’d give it a one star rating no matter what redeemable qualities I’d find in it. But as I read more and actually started thinking about it, I realised there are books that aren’t even worthy of that single star, books that are, to me, beneath contempt. To compensate, I adjusted my personal rating scale and now one star is reserved to books that induce burning white rage in me. <br /><br />I’ve given good ratings to books with characters I’ve hated when I enjoyed the story, and I’ve given good ratings to books with stories I’ve hated even when I loved a character or two. For me, the style matters little, but dammit, it matters.<br /><br />And I’m not talking about the clunky language that in a way fits the subject and the legend, but takes a while to get used to. <br /><br />Ms. Bradley set out to write a retelling of the Arthurian legend from the female perspective, and in that she succeeded. She managed to put together a logical and a somewhat coherent version of the events that put King Arthur on his throne in Camelot and brought him down from it, and she managed to tell it with female voices. Igraine, Viviane, Morgaine, Gwenhwyfar, Morgause, all these women claw their way from the footnotes of the myth and become three dimensional people—not just characters, but people—with worries and joys of their own. <br /><br />Admittedly those joys were short-lived, but that’s partly why I loved the story. It’s why I love the legend as I do all things heart-rending.<br /><br />However, as wonderfully flawed all these people were with their virtues and their unbridled ambitions, none of them really had a choice in the matter. Ms. Bradley didn’t write people, women or men, who made the best of their unfortunate circumstances. She wrote people thrown about by the fates and whims of their deities. Morgaine’s last defence is that she never had a choice and that she was merely the Goddess’ instrument. <br /><br />And that’s why I hate this book. <br /><br />All the characters, as Ms. Bradley paints them, are passive. None are active. None make choices and then take responsibility for their actions. They’re all thrown into untenable situations where something must break and either give them that what they most wish or take it all away from them. <br /><br />Igraine marries because she doesn’t have a choice. She goes to convent, because she can’t bear to face the sister who forced her hand. <br /><br />Gwenhwyfar also marries, because she doesn’t have a choice. She first surrenders to her lover because she doesn’t have a choice. The only stupid choice she makes is so that the author has an excuse to make the pious lady into an adulteress without making her <em>choose</em> it.<br /><br />Morgaine, the worst offender, chooses nothing. The closest she comes to making up her own mind is when she flees Avalon, but after that she promptly becomes the meekest of them all. She, who should be the fearsome Lady of the Lake and High Priestess of the Goddess, how can she be a vehicle of her Goddess’ will when she does nothing but allows others act around her? <br /><br />Catalyst, you say? This isn’t a chemical reaction where one substance remains unchanged. People change, people make choices that change them and others around them. Unless, of course, you’re a character in <em>The Mists of Avalon.</em><br /><br />But times were different then and women nothing but chattel, you say? There’s difference in being victimised and being a victim. All Morgaine and the others had to do to win me over, was not to see themselves as victims. All they had to do was to endure what was thrown at them and choose to make the best of it. All they had to do was to choose. <br /><br />Only Morgause and Viviane come close to choosing anything, and how are their choices rewarded? Why of course, <em>they</em> are the great villainesses whose actions lead to a family tragedy after a family tragedy. Their actions bring an end to all those things they love and they don’t live to see the aftermath or acknowledge their responsibility. <br /><br />Telling a story from the female perspective doesn’t make it feminist; writing capable women doing things, being active, and making choices does. This book is something worse; it’s a pretender. <br /><br />There are many things I appreciate in this book, one thing I don’t is how it all was told. That matters. Dammit.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1639863693668981006">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_661766679">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/661766679" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/6450088-tania" class="left imgcol" title="Tania"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1451883358p2/6450088.jpg" alt="Tania" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/661766679?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 12, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6450088-tania" class="user" title="Tania">Tania</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Tania by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/1456717-barbara-kling">Barbara Kling</a>
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/6450088-tania?shelf=favorite-books" class="actionLinkLite">favorite-books</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6450088-tania?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer661766679">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16396549529073066599">Once again I have to hang my head in shame and admit that I knew next to nothing about King Arthur, his Knights of the Round Table and Camelot before reading this. The Mists of Avalon was probably not the best book to start with as this presents us with an alternative version of this well-known legend.<br /><br />I really enjoyed this book as it was a mixture of two of my favorite author’s, <a title="Philipa Gregory" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7079129.Philipa_Gregory">Philipa Gregory</a>(all about the intrigues at court) and <a title="Juliet Marillier" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8649.Juliet_Marillier">Juliet Marillier</a>(although the fantasy aspect in MoA is of a more</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16396549529073066599">Once again I have to hang my head in shame and admit that I knew next to nothing about King Arthur, his Knights of the Round Table and Camelot before reading this. The Mists of Avalon was probably not the best book to start with as this presents us with an alternative version of this well-known legend.<br /><br />I really enjoyed this book as it was a mixture of two of my favorite author’s, <a title="Philipa Gregory" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7079129.Philipa_Gregory">Philipa Gregory</a>(all about the intrigues at court) and <a title="Juliet Marillier" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8649.Juliet_Marillier">Juliet Marillier</a>(although the fantasy aspect in MoA is of a more adult nature).<br /><br />The story is told from an all female perspective – Igraine, Vivianne, Morgause, Morgaine and Gwenhwyfar. They are all very strong and fully rounded characters. So much so that no-one is cast as a heroine or villainess. Everyone is their own biggest enemy, and you will find yourself loving and hating all of them at times. I would have liked to have more insight into the male characters. At the end of the book I walked away still feeling that I didn’t really understand Arthur and Lancelet. <br /><br />The main theme in this novel is Brittan’s move from the old ways/paganism to Christianity. Although other books I’ve read have touched on this, I’ve never realized what a difficult process this must have been – not only for the country as a whole, but also on a personal level. The opposing religions are represented by Morgaine la Faye and Gwenhwyfar, who are both fanatical in trying to protect their beliefs. I loved how Taliesin, the Merlin, was able to balance these opposing views in his life.<br /><br />So yes, this is a long, sometimes dark tale of people you won’t always like – but it totally enchanted me to the point where I struggled to tear myself away from it. I am now also very interested in reading more about this legend – if you have any recommendations, please let me know.<br /><br /><blockquote>Do you think that all our sorcery could bring about anything other than God's will, my child.<blockquote><br /></blockquote>
</blockquote></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16396549529073066599">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_824008561">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/824008561" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/13522217-leah-williams" class="left imgcol" title="Leah Williams"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1409088394p2/13522217.jpg" alt="Leah Williams" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/824008561?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 29, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/13522217-leah-williams" class="user" title="Leah Williams">Leah Williams</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/13522217-leah-williams?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer824008561">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7232878725508380752">This is a feminist work. I saw a few one-star reviews (from dudes AND ladies) of this saying that the women were boring or slutty or whatever coded misogyny nonsense, but let me get something off my chest: do not confuse "having strong female characters" with "female badass fetishization" because this book absolutely has the former. <br /><br />The women were strong and they were complex and each one of them had this beautifully woven narrative. Feminine =\= unfeminist. Spinning, weaving, childbirth, mother</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7232878725508380752">This is a feminist work. I saw a few one-star reviews (from dudes AND ladies) of this saying that the women were boring or slutty or whatever coded misogyny nonsense, but let me get something off my chest: do not confuse "having strong female characters" with "female badass fetishization" because this book absolutely has the former. <br /><br />The women were strong and they were complex and each one of them had this beautifully woven narrative. Feminine =\= unfeminist. Spinning, weaving, childbirth, motherhood, sex, periods, heartbreaks, first uncomfortable pangs of romance- these are all honest and authentic experiences of these women. <br /><br />The characters navigated their world, insular as it may have been, in a manner accommodating the men who ran it. Behind the scenes and pulling strings, that's what these women were doing. Standing close to the spotlight and never stepping in it. <br /><br />I thought there was a beautiful symmetry in this book- once I got to the end and all the scattered pieces started to come together again (because yo, not gonna lie, this book will wander far and wide from the original starting point), it felt like this bellowing crescendo to me. Hallowed moments of tender mercies and divine revelations finally knit back together and shaped this incredible feminist narrative of women and God. <br /><br />Here's a backstory: I have a "Valar Morghulis" tattoo. I love asoiaf unconditionally and forgive GRRM being unable to write women's anatomy. He writes women like they were men, and I appreciate the complexity this offers women roles. While I was reading Mists of Avalon I thought of Gregory Macguire's Wicked novels and how Elphaba, like Morgaine, eventually wanders into moral grey areas and makes mistakes. Elphaba hardens, she resigns herself to wickedness and coldness and keeps her vulnerability hidden. Addendum to backstory: I'm not religious in the slightest. <br /><br />But now I know what a complex woman in a fantasy setting looks like when written by a woman and I am never going back. Reading female characters who show strength as well as vulnerability? Fortitude and weakness? How refreshing is this, reading women who aren't written as men or earn their girlpower mantle by wielding swords and acting like men? I have never had a particularly favorable attitude towards Christianity and have kept a respectful and silent distance, but the end of this book brought about a new affection for how beautiful Christianity can be. I was deeply moved by this book.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7232878725508380752">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
