<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_62838856">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/62838856" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2447325-dawn-ron" class="left imgcol" title="Dawn (&amp; Ron)"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1336267796p2/2447325.jpg" alt="Dawn (&amp; Ron)" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/62838856?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 09, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2447325-dawn-ron" class="user" title="Dawn (&amp; Ron)">Dawn (&amp; Ron)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2447325-dawn-ron?shelf=historical-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">historical-fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2447325-dawn-ron?shelf=two-fer-read" class="actionLinkLite">two-fer-read</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2447325-dawn-ron?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2447325-dawn-ron?shelf=arthurian" class="actionLinkLite">arthurian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer62838856">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1264845829160304155">When Ron and I read this book, it turned out to be one of those rare occasions where our opinions were diversely opposed. Of course, there are always things from our shared reads, where we disagree, but overall our combined consensus is relatively the same. Not with <em>The Mists of Avalon</em>! We could find no common ground. <br /><br />Originally Ron had picked this out for himself, I wasn't even part of the equation when originally purchased. That is until he started to read the book. The more he read, the more</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1264845829160304155">When Ron and I read this book, it turned out to be one of those rare occasions where our opinions were diversely opposed. Of course, there are always things from our shared reads, where we disagree, but overall our combined consensus is relatively the same. Not with <em>The Mists of Avalon</em>! We could find no common ground. <br /><br />Originally Ron had picked this out for himself, I wasn't even part of the equation when originally purchased. That is until he started to read the book. The more he read, the more he kept telling me that maybe I should read it. The more he read, the less he liked it, to the point where he did not finish it and just couldn't go any further, another major rarity. <br /><br />He reads a lot of fantasy, is used to female centric main characters and is quite used to chunksters, so it should have been a nice fit. It never fit right for him. It just came across as too female for him, not girly-girl mind you, just too much female angst and feelings for him.<br /><br />Why did I read it if he disliked it so much? Well, to see what the heck he was complaining about and to see if he was right that this was more of a woman's read. I never even intended to read the book, I'd never even read a fantasy before. Even then, I didn't plan to read it, just peruse it some. That was a pleasant mistake, because I couldn't put it down, I had to read every page.<br /><br />Immediately, I fell under it's evocative spell. Yes, it's the tales of Arthur from the female point of view. I won't go into a summary, so many others have already provided that. These are such compelling, intriguing, loving, manipulative women, every emotion a woman could go through is explored here. But these are complex, multidimensional woman, not to be taken lightly. Instead sit back and enjoy the adventure, romance, betrayals and magic. <br /><br />Bradley weaves a powerful tale, you can feel the mist over the lake clinging to you, feel the sharp, brilliant edge of the blade as you wield it. You participate in pagan rituals while being appalled at the same time and you want to yell out, stop, please don't do it. That's how invested you become in their lives. <br /><br />Admittedly, it can get a little slow in some places but these are minor and easily forgotten when under its narrative thrall. Some scenes sexual frankness and forbidden overtones may make some uncomfortable. Also Christians are treated almost as sheep, easily lead but not being able to think for themselves. These scenes make up a very small portion but for those sensitive to these issues, I wanted to point them out.<br /><br />This is a visceral, tangible Camelot, yet at the same time, an intangible, ethereal, unattainable Camelot. How Bradley walks that fine line is the true magic of this book.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1264845829160304155">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1442311655459439205">I spent a good deal of time living in this book, and I find I am haunted and saddened that my time with Morgaine is over. Some of the critical reviews point out the novel's flaws (over-long and repetitive) and that is very much a truth......but, oh, what magic it was to get lost in Avalon's world of mysticism and the spirituality of the Great Goddess. An imaginative retelling of the legendary King Arthur and the conflict of paganism and Christianity told from the point of view of the priestess o</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1442311655459439205">I spent a good deal of time living in this book, and I find I am haunted and saddened that my time with Morgaine is over. Some of the critical reviews point out the novel's flaws (over-long and repetitive) and that is very much a truth......but, oh, what magic it was to get lost in Avalon's world of mysticism and the spirituality of the Great Goddess.   <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown">
    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1320883816">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1320883816" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/18438835-callum" class="left imgcol" title="Callum"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1381804049p2/18438835.jpg" alt="Callum" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1320883816?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 29, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/18438835-callum" class="user" title="Callum">Callum</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1320883816">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12756077337468508070">I remember reading the condensed versions of Sir Thomas Malory's Le Morte d'Arthur and enjoying them thoroughly. Reading The Mists of Avalon, I was slightly alienated by the cold, unromantic approach Bradley decided on in retelling the legend, swapping out the mysticism of Merlin, The Lady of The Lake and the evil sorceress Morgause and instead implanting them with the magic of neo-pagan theology. <br /><br />Also at cost of Bradley's approach was the sweeping and winsome tales of Arthur and his knights of</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12756077337468508070">I remember reading the condensed versions of Sir Thomas Malory's Le Morte d'Arthur and enjoying them thoroughly. Reading The Mists of Avalon, I was slightly alienated by the cold, unromantic approach Bradley decided on in retelling the legend, swapping out the mysticism of Merlin, The Lady of The Lake and the evil sorceress Morgause and instead implanting them with the magic of neo-pagan theology. <br /><br />Also at cost of Bradley's approach was the sweeping and winsome tales of Arthur and his knights of the round table, and replaced with instead a more political, Machiavellian take on the women of King Arthur. Despite the odd nature Bradley brings to her retelling, I found this worth the read. The women of King Arthur are certainly empowered, but are not exemplified as better than men in any way, instead Mists of Avalon reads more as a fable on the consequences of power and the accompanying tragedy that comes with keeping it.<br /><br />The subversion of classic Arthurian myth are mostly hit and miss here. The anti-Christian rhetoric is ever-present, told mostly through the voice of Gwenhwyfar (Guinevere) who is negatively portrayed as Arthur's manipulator and the destruction of Avalon's influence in Arthur's court. Despite Gwenhwyfar being portrayed as a religious fanatic however, I would say Bradley portrays her with enough dignity to justify her as more of a victim of her oppressive upbringing. Mordred, who I've come to see as the embodiment of treachery and evil in these Arthurian tales, is ultimately more humanized here but no less presents the story with a Manichaean conflict that resolves this Arthur saga in a satisfying, complete way.<br /><br />Bradley would sometimes become a little silly and self-contradictory in the way she would write classic Arthurian stories; for example, in place of the Holy Grail is a magic Druid chalice that places everyone who drinks from it in a blissful high, leading to a massive delusion that ultimately results in the hapless and doomed quest for it. And of course the story is rife with pagan magic and Druid sorceries which are of course portrayed with more fervour and respect than they probably deserve, considering that this version of King Arthur seems to de-emphasize classic Arthurian mysticism . . .<br /><br />Overall, Bradley writes an engaging tale, full of revelation, foreboding, character introspection and political intrigue. Her ideas of "what is or what should be" are at times rather didactic and present ideas that seem to be in favour of Bradley's theological underpinnings, as opposed to ideas that can be interpreted by the reader. That being said, I am not completely against most of Bradley's ideas; she does offer a progressive and otherwise more pro-educative view on theology. One that inspires thought and rumination on the power, disillusionment and manipulation of a belief system. Her portrayal of women, particularly the ones who hold influence over the men, is interesting but heavy-handed and not entirely convincing, even in the logic of the Britain that she has created.<br /><br />So how does this neo-pagan, anti-Christian, woman-dominated, wallowing existential fable of an Arthurian retelling hold-up? Quite adequately.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12756077337468508070">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_41142543">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/41142543" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1838992-gregory" class="left imgcol" title="Gregory"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" alt="Gregory" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/41142543?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 01, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1838992-gregory" class="user" title="Gregory">Gregory</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1838992-gregory?shelf=historical-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1838992-gregory?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer41142543">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10722236163964534035">What can I say about this book? I understand that this is largely considered to be one of the great classics of modern fantasy literature. But personally, I found it to be a tedious, repetitive, grossly innaccurate affair that has little redeeming value. To be fair, I have to applaud Bradley for the sheer audacity of what she attempts to accomplish with this book: it's not an easy job re-conceiving the vast array of Arthurian legends. Perhaps she merely bit off a lot more than she could chew. Bu</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10722236163964534035">What can I say about this book? I understand that this is largely considered to be one of the great classics of modern fantasy literature. But personally, I found it to be a tedious, repetitive, grossly innaccurate affair that has little redeeming value. To be fair, I have to applaud Bradley for the sheer audacity of what she attempts to accomplish with this book: it's not an easy job re-conceiving the vast array of Arthurian legends. Perhaps she merely bit off a lot more than she could chew. But nevertheless, this book wound up being one of the great disappointments of my reading career.<br /><br />The entire point of Bradley's book seems to be not really to tell a story, but to push a neo-feminist, neo-pagan point of view. Her arguments, that Christianity ruined egalitarian earth-loving Celtic cultures and shackled women under a male-dominated cultural power supported by the Church, are repetitive and monotonous. Her book becomes far more concerned with repeating this argument over and over again, and the plot suffers greatly. Even if you do agree with some of her points (which I do), you find yourself becoming quite aggravated by her tiresome diatribes well before the book is half finished. Any basic writing instructor will tell you that when setting out to write a story, don't <em>try</em> to make a point. If your writing is good enough, it will make that point clear for you, while leaving your reader to determine their own conclusions. Obviously, Bradley never got this piece of advice or simply chose to ignore it.<br /><br />The other grave fault of the books is that Bradley's perspective is based on a lot of New Age, Neo-pagan pseudo-history than any real research. Either she didn't know any better, or just as likely, she chose to ignore historical fact. Now I can allow for a healthy amount of artistic interpretation to history, especially when you take into account the Arthurian period, which is itself layered in so much myth and speculation. But Bradley goes beyond the acceptable levels of "stretching the facts," and instead weaves such blatant misrepresentations that it makes one cringe. For one, Celtic cultures were hardly the peaceful, egalitarian, feminist examples that Bradley portrays them to be. Their religious organization was male-dominated, and they engaged in human sacrifice and even ritual rape. In her attempts to color Christianity black she entirely overlooks the contributions the Church made in bringing peace to war-torn Britain. And, perhaps her most horrendous and unforgiveable sin, is in her portrayal of St. Patrick, who becomes Arthur's bishop in the later half of the book. Not only did Patrick never become a bishop in Britain (or hold any real post there whatsoever), but Bradley again overlooks the fact that while in Ireland on his mission of conversion, Patrick actually allowed for female bishops and priests and created perhaps one of the most egalitarian versions of the Catholic Church.<br /><br />These points aisde, what about her actual portrayal of the Arthurian legends? Bradley's characters are mostly one-dimensional, alas. There is very little narrative structure, and most of the "action" of the novel occurs within the characters. As mentioned, she seems a lot more interested in making her socio-philosophical point than in telling a real story. The great events of the Arthurian tales are mostly glossed over, though she does have some interesting and intriguing re-interpretations of some of the episode. Unfortunately, these are few, and the end of the book is especially anti-climactic. Arthur's tragic death and the dissolution of the dream of Camelot is merely a footnote.<br /><br />The final verdict? Read this book if, like me, you are very interested in the Arthurian cycles and their re-interpretations throughout history. This is one of those works that, while painful to plod through, should at least be attempted in order to gain a better understanding of the modern impact of Arthur and his exploits. However, beyond that cultural context, this book hardly stands out. If you are looking for a unique, intriguing, and multidimensional treatment of the Arthurian legends, then I recommend you seek out T.H. White's <em>The Once and Future King</em>.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10722236163964534035">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_16773082">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/16773082" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/745969-chelsea" class="left imgcol" title="Chelsea"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1271730893p2/745969.jpg" alt="Chelsea" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/16773082?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 01, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/745969-chelsea" class="user" title="Chelsea">Chelsea</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer16773082">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7527754154535534550">It took me two whole months to get through this 876 page tome. Not that it wasn't two months well spent, but in the scheme of things, even with my slow pace of late, two months is a long time.<br /><br />MZB's well crafted world of Avalon and Camelot is not a bad place to spend two months; I actually quite enjoyed the book, up until the last hundred pages or so. She creates a rich tapestry of characters and circumstances (one of those books that probably needs a map and family tree in the back, but hasn't.)</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7527754154535534550">It took me two whole months to get through this 876 page tome. Not that it wasn't two months well spent, but in the scheme of things, even with my slow pace of late, two months is a long time.<br /><br />MZB's well crafted world of Avalon and Camelot is not a bad place to spend two months; I actually quite enjoyed the book, up until the last hundred pages or so. She creates a rich tapestry of characters and circumstances (one of those books that probably needs a map and family tree in the back, but hasn't.) Generally, I'm not a big fan of books that have to give you character maps, but because of the epic nature of the novel, you're introduced to the characters gradually, over time as they move in and out of the story, and some die off, some are born, so you're not trying to keep all of their stories straight at the same time. <br /><br />There are times when the time line itself gets a little confusing, simply because of the effort to tell all these congruent tales. Ultimately, they blend together pretty well into something that's pretty readable and often captivating, if sometimes a little wordy. (Like fantasy tends to do.)<br /><br />Something I'll only mention briefly is the issues of sexuality/sexism that are addressed through the characters lives. Bradley creates women who are strong-willed, born into a tradition of matriarchal hierarchies and yet, when brought into the increasingly Christian court of Arthur, they seem to meld themselves right into the "traditional" female roles - sitting in gossipy group, spinning and weaving all day. Kinda frustrating. Stop whining about how you want to be included in the court talk and go down there, dammit! <br /><br />As I mentioned earlier, my biggest problem with the book is the end, really. In the last coupl of chapters, the whole story seems to dissolve into mists and uncertainties; suddenly a lot of people are dying. The main character, who's spent the majority of her time being very strong willed and self-assured starts to break down into mush. The religious talk gets very mealy-mouthed in the Druid/Christian/Pagan debate. Overall, it just sort of felt like she spent the first 700 pages creating this great world, and then realized in the last 100 that she had to destroy it somehow to get back to "reality". <br /><br />All told, I really like her retelling of the Camelot story. Her characters are real, and the three-somes/pagan sex rituals are kept to a tasteful minimum. I do have to say that its length worked against it in one respect - it took me so long to read that I was kinda wanting to be done and move on to the next thing about two thirds of the way through. So, if you're a quick reader, or you find yourself with lots of free time on your hands, I would recommend this classic fantasy novel. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7527754154535534550">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_19551661">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/19551661" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/86892-letitia" class="left imgcol" title="Letitia"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1402326273p2/86892.jpg" alt="Letitia" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/19551661?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 11, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/86892-letitia" class="user" title="Letitia">Letitia</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Patient Feminists and Pagans</span></div>

        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer19551661">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17489099185916978514">It is incredibly difficult to carry a reader's interest through a nearly 900 page novel...and Marion Zimmer Bradly does no better than most. <br /><br />I was fascinated by the perspective of this novel from the first page. It is essentially a retelling of the Arthur legend from the female perspective, giving all of the characters an entirely new slant. This was much appreciated, but when you have nothing to say, can only carry a book so far. What might have been great plot was far too often distracted by m</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17489099185916978514">It is incredibly difficult to carry a reader's interest through a nearly 900 page novel...and Marion Zimmer Bradly does no better than most. <br /><br />I was fascinated by the perspective of this novel from the first page. It is essentially a retelling of the Arthur legend from the female perspective, giving all of the characters an entirely new slant. This was much appreciated, but when you have nothing to say, can only carry a book so far. What might have been great plot was far too often distracted by meaningless liaisons, and a redundancy that was maddenning.<br /><br />My biggest beef with MZB was an odd marriage of repetiveness and inconsistency. She could not seem to decide whether her characters were beautiful or homely, aged or perpetually youthful, strident or shrinking, gay or straight. Her most consistent character was Gwenhwyfar, and by the end of the novel the imagined whine of her voice in my head was enough to cause me to skip passages at a time. This coupled with MZB's propensity to repeat facts and re-introduce a well-hashed conflict repeatedly left me frustrated with both her structure and style. I do not need to be reminded eighty-seven times during the course of your novel that Lancelet is handsome, dark, slender, and athletic. I get it. Her almost obsessive reminders about the conflicts between the belief systems of Christianity and those of Avalon were maddening. In spite of her seeming sympathy to the pagan perspective, she could not seem to consistently yet compassionately portray the religion. It goes from being a faith of torturous duty to pure pleasure, absolute asceticism to hedonism, a haughty disdain for any symbolic object to a death threat when holy relics are stolen from the isle of Avalon. Essentialy, MZB could not seem to get her story straight, and she didn't have enough story to fill 900 pages to begin with, so it can hardly be expected that one would write a truly significant novel with that level of inconsistency.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17489099185916978514">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_3906525">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3906525" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/236894-dayna" class="left imgcol" title="Dayna"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1223246233p2/236894.jpg" alt="Dayna" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/3906525?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 27, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/236894-dayna" class="user" title="Dayna">Dayna</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer3906525">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14924133561390615399">I commented before that this book is slogging ... and that's what it is. I slogged through half of it ... and then it was due back at the library (yeah ... it took me a month to slog through half of it) so I skimmed the rest until the end. I'm going to be fair and say that if you enjoy Arthurian romance and if you already know the legends through and through that you might enjoy The Mists of Avalon more than I did. Yet you may be even more frustrated than I was. Perhaps disgusted. I cannot give</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14924133561390615399">I commented before that this book is slogging ... and that's what it is. I slogged through half of it ... and then it was due back at the library (yeah ... it took me a month to slog through half of it) so I skimmed the rest until the end. I'm going to be fair and say that if you enjoy Arthurian romance and if you already know the legends through and through that you might enjoy The Mists of Avalon more than I did. Yet you may be even more frustrated than I was. Perhaps disgusted. I cannot give it less than two stars (even though I think it's really only a one-star) simply because of the fact that if I understood all of the author's sources I might have liked it a lot more than I did. As it is I just could not get into it and so I call it second-rate slop. Tripe. <br /><br />It is the story of Arthur and Camelot from the womens' point of view. Sounds excellent, right? Whatever. I was frustrated by the fact that every female character seemed manic. In one paragraph she (any SHE in the story) is madly in love with a man (any MAN in the story) and in the next ... moments later SHE is digusted with him. Back and forth it goes. One minute SHE feels like a goddess and the next she feels lost and all alone and confused. Arthur is basically pathetic and all his knights are either "My sword is bigger than yours!" or "I love Pathetic Arthur more than you do!" Beyond that it seems like the book could have been cut by half and you would have had the same exact story ... with all of its wishy-washy characters. Give me a break! I basically concluded that some people will like the "feminine" aspect better than I did and that I simply prefer the story of Arthur to be told from the warrior aspects. Forget the twisted love crap. I wanted more Excalibur! And not this "the blood of a woman woven into the threads of the sheath ... " blah-blah-blah. Just the sword &amp; battle ... and the knights' adventures. I don't care who so-and-so may or may not have fooled around with. Anywho ... if you're into King Arthur I would not recommend this book to get you started. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14924133561390615399">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_316468516">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/316468516" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2190064-nenia-campbell" class="left imgcol" title="Nenia Campbell"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1435654700p2/2190064.jpg" alt="Nenia Campbell" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/316468516?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 06, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2190064-nenia-campbell" class="user" title="Nenia Campbell">Nenia Campbell</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2190064-nenia-campbell?shelf=trollers-gonna-troll" class="actionLinkLite">trollers-gonna-troll</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2190064-nenia-campbell?shelf=kool-aid-s-not-my-thing-baby" class="actionLinkLite">kool-aid-s-not-my-thing-baby</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer316468516">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2232419112994769170"><strong>Feminist</strong> fiction you say?<br /><br /><span>[image error]</span><br /><br />Oh, book. How do you piss me off? Let me count the ways.<br /><br />1. You try to masquerade as a strong female fantasy novel when your MC is a total bitch who spends all her time debating over whether or not to give her husband permission when he ravishes her or not (because <strong>it's not rape if you're married</strong>, folks!).<br /><br />2. The MC's first husband sleeps around, but she's totally OK with that - until he starts giving her younger sister The Eye. Oh, but god help the MC if she</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2232419112994769170"><strong>Feminist</strong> fiction you say?<br /><br /><span>[image error]</span><br /><br />Oh, book. How do you piss me off? Let me count the ways.<br /><br />1. You try to masquerade as a strong female fantasy novel when your MC is a total bitch who spends all her time debating over whether or not to give her husband permission when he ravishes her or not (because <strong>it's not rape if you're married</strong>, folks!).<br /><br />2. The MC's first husband sleeps around, but she's totally OK with that - until he starts giving her younger sister The Eye. Oh, but god help the MC if she ever decides to cheat. When she even looks at a guy wrong her husband comes close to beating and then raping her.<br /><br />3. Borderline pedophilia. Because if she's got her monthlies, you can give it to her daily, apparently.<br /><br />4. Merlin is a pimp in this novel. No, seriously. He <em>is</em>. "Cheat on your husband - he's going to be dead anyway soon and this guy is a total stud. Plus, who's the whore and who's the wizard here? Exactly. NOW SLEEP WITH HIM YOU SLUT. AND BEAR THE SON I NEED FOR MY PROPHECY!"<br /><br />5. He's not a rapist. He just didn't know how to deal with the young beautiful girl he was saddled with as a wife. As soon as she stopped fighting him and calling it "rape" he was a perfectly nice man. No, that's not Stockholm Syndrome at all. It's marriage.<br /><br /><span>[image error]</span><br /><br />I mean, <strong>seriously</strong>. Why on earth is this touted as being a feminist work of fantasy? It is so <em>not</em>. <a title="Ella Enchanted by Gail Carson Levine" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24337.Ella_Enchanted">Ella Enchanted</a>? Yes. <a title="Tamora Pierce" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8596.Tamora_Pierce">Tamora Pierce</a>? Yes. <a title="Just Ella by Margaret Peterson Haddix" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/162719.Just_Ella">Just Ella</a>? Yes. <a title="The True Confessions of Charlotte Doyle by Avi" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/310146.The_True_Confessions_of_Charlotte_Doyle">The True Confessions of Charlotte Doyle</a>? Yes. <a title="The Witch of Blackbird Pond by Elizabeth George Speare" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/703292.The_Witch_of_Blackbird_Pond">The Witch of Blackbird Pond</a>? Yes (although that fragging character annoys the heck out of me).<br /><br /><a title="The Mists of Avalon (Avalon, #1) by Marion Zimmer Bradley" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/402045.The_Mists_of_Avalon__Avalon___1_">The Mists of Avalon</a>? <strong>No</strong>.<br /><br />It's not even particularly well-written, either. I found it very heavy-handed and full of purple prose. The words were so tangled up in their own sense of self-importance that I found myself forgetting the plot so many times that I eventually just stopped caring.<br /><br />Plus, all the rape and girly things and rampant sexism really grated my cheese, if you know what I mean. I just can't read a book that makes me want to do this:<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380375676ra/730308.gif" /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2232419112994769170">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_12013591">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/12013591" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/759103-vera" class="left imgcol" title="Vera"><img src="http://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" alt="Vera" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/12013591?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 08, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/759103-vera" class="user" title="Vera">Vera</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">anyone interested in medieval lit/feminism, or who loves the movie Practical Magic</span></div>

        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer12013591">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2925740266647919703">I watched the miniseries version all in one night with my old roommate, and loved it. I also really liked the concept of re-telling (pretty much re-writing) the Arthurian legends from the point of view of the women. <br /><br />I was fascinated by some things in this book - the way of life and beliefs of the people living "the old way" on the mystical isle of Avalon (pagan, I think?), following the Goddess, contrasted against the new Christian views of the people at Arthur's court. Also, the old well-trodde</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2925740266647919703">I watched the miniseries version all in one night with my old roommate, and loved it. I also really liked the concept of re-telling (pretty much re-writing) the Arthurian legends from the point of view of the women. <br /><br />I was fascinated by some things in this book - the way of life and beliefs of the people living "the old way" on the mystical isle of Avalon (pagan, I think?), following the Goddess, contrasted against the new Christian views of the people at Arthur's court. Also, the old well-trodden stories are told from such a different perspective - sometimes almost as an afterthought to what seems like the primary action - that they're made brand-new. At times I didn't even recognize them until part-way through. Being enamored with medieval literature, I loved this and giggled like a nerd when I realized the old, unwieldy table that Guinevere's father gave Arthur as a wedding present (mentioned offhandedly) was to become the infamous Round Table.<br /><br />The book gets a bit slow at times when many pages are spent on events at Court, social courtesies, or discussions at dinner. But overall, it's incredibly romantic. I definitely developed a crush on Lancelet (Lancelot) and Morgaine's lover - can't remember his name. The characters are excellent, tragically flawed (if somewhat annoying at times). Gwenhwyfar (Guinevere) is an infuriating character that you love to hate. And it's worth making it all the way through this VERY long book for the poignant, perfect ending.<br /><br />What I loved the most was the portrayal of the rites and powers of Avalon... and the way the land of Avalon could only be found by certain people (sometimes by accident!!) in the mists. It simultaneously occupied the same island as Glastonbury, where nuns and priests lived, but was somehow on the other side of a veil.... eventually "lost to time." GREAT stuff.<br /><br />It also showed a very different kind of female power, a spiritual/sensual one that's tied to the land and life itself - kind of similar to what's described in The Da Vinci Code. <br /><br />Two of my favorite quotes: "Yours is the face of the Goddess for me."<br /><br />"Truth has many faces and the truth is like to the old road to Avalon; it depends on your own will and your own thoughts, whither the road will take you."<br /><br />I look forward to re-reading this book sometime!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2925740266647919703">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&amp;book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;refresh=true' + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('jdNhlWaGGfDIo6BU+NOk3ltDAfJnhZN1C5htCS+1vdboB15GlFQNCkR71O+GskJsErTC6y5Y1N9hkWib4yBLkw==')})
  }
//]]&gt;
</script>

If you\'re Jack Whyte, you begin your retelling of the Arthurian saga by taking one giant step backward to the latter days of the Roman Empire in Britain, sometime between the first breaching of Hadrian\'s Wall and the legendary days of King Arthur. Publius Varrus is the last legionnaire in Britain, and The Skystone is in many ways his story. He is a common man with aristocratic friends, and successful both as a soldier and an ironsmith. As the Roman world slowly crumbles around them, and Publius becomes involved in a political and personal vendetta, he and his friends seek to establish a refuge, a valley where the old Roman virtues will be kept alive and the empire\'s many faults be avoided.  A finely crafted historical novel, The Skystone pays close attention to the details of everyday life in fourth-century Britain. As the first book in Whyte\'s Camulod Chronicles, it makes few allusions to the usual details of the Arthurian legends until Publius comes into contact with a sword, a stone, a lake, and a Celtic tribe who name themselves Pendragon.     <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4841825.Marion_Zimmer_Bradley">About Marion Zimmer Bradley</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/4841825.Marion_Zimmer_Bradley"><img alt="4841825" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1305483488p2/4841825.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer15916740695875855594">Marion Eleanor Zimmer Bradley was an American author of fantasy novels such as The Mists of Avalon and the Darkover series, often with a feminist outlook.<br /><br />Bradley's first published novel-length work was <em>Falcons of Narabedla</em>, first published in the May 1957 issue of <strong>Other Worlds</strong>. When she was a child, Bradley stated that she enjoyed reading adventure fantasy authors such as Henry Kuttner, Edmond Ham</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15916740695875855594">Marion Eleanor Zimmer Bradley was an American author of fantasy novels such as The Mists of Avalon and the Darkover series, often with a feminist outlook.<br /><br />Bradley's first published novel-length work was <em>Falcons of Narabedla</em>, first published in the May 1957 issue of <strong>Other Worlds</strong>. When she was a child, Bradley stated that she enjoyed reading adventure fantasy authors such as Henry Kuttner, Edmond Hamilton, and Leigh Brackett, especially when they wrote about "the glint of strange suns on worlds that never were and never would be." Her first novel and much of her subsequent work show their influence strongly.<br /><br />Early in her career, writing as Morgan Ives, Miriam Gardner, John Dexter, and Lee Chapman, Marion Zimmer Bradley produced several works outside the speculative fiction genre, including some gay and lesbian pulp fiction novels. For example, <em>I Am a Lesbian</em> was published in 1962. Though relatively tame by today's standards, they were considered pornographic when published, and for a long time she refused to disclose the titles she wrote under these pseudonyms.<br /><br />Her 1958 story "The Planet Savers" introduced the planet of Darkover, which became the setting of a popular series by Bradley and other authors. The Darkover milieu may be considered as either fantasy with science fiction overtones or as science fiction with fantasy overtones, as Darkover is a lost earth colony where psi powers developed to an unusual degree. Bradley wrote many Darkover novels by herself, but in her later years collaborated with other authors for publication; her literary collaborators have continued the series since her death.<br /><br />Bradley took an active role in science-fiction and fantasy fandom, promoting interaction with professional authors and publishers and making several important contributions to the subculture.<br /><br />For many years, Bradley actively encouraged Darkover fan fiction and reprinted some of it in commercial Darkover anthologies, continuing to encourage submissions from unpublished authors, but this ended after a dispute with a fan over an unpublished Darkover novel of Bradley's that had similarities to some of the fan's stories. As a result, the novel remained unpublished, and Bradley demanded the cessation of all Darkover fan fiction.<br /><br />Bradley was also the editor of the long-running <em>Sword and Sorceress</em> anthology series, which encouraged submissions of fantasy stories featuring original and non-traditional heroines from young and upcoming authors. Although she particularly encouraged young female authors, she was not averse to including male authors in her anthologies. Mercedes Lackey was just one of many authors who first appeared in the anthologies. She also maintained a large family of writers at her home in Berkeley. Ms Bradley was editing the final <em>Sword and Sorceress</em> manuscript up until the week of her death in September of 1999.<br /><br />Probably her most famous single novel is <em>The Mists of Avalon</em>. A retelling of the Camelot legend from the point of view of Morgaine and Gwenhwyfar, it grew into a series of books; like the Darkover series, the later novels are written with or by other authors and have continued to appear after Bradley's death.<br /><br />In 2000, she was posthumously awarded the World Fantasy Award for Lifetime Achievement. <br /><br />In 2014, Bradley was accused of sexual abuse by her daughter, Moira Greyland, who claims that she was molested from the age of 3 to 12. Greyland also claimed that she was not the only victim and that she was one of the people who reported her father, Walter H. Breen, for child molestation. In response to these allegations Bradley's publisher Victor Gollancz Ltd announced that they will donate all income from the sales of Bradley's e-books to the charity Save the Children.<br /><br />- <em>From Wikipedia</em></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15916740695875855594">...more</a>

    </div>

Marion Zimmer Bradley?s Ravens of Avalon is the prequel to The Forest House and tells the story of the Roman conquest of Britain and the origin of the Forest House that preceded the return to Avalon. Marion Zimmer Bradley?s Ravens of Avalon follows the journey of Boudica, a Celtic princess, and Lhiannon, a priestess who is Boudica?s mentor on the Druid isle. When the Romans conquer Britain, Lhiannon fights them while Boudica is married to a king who has surrendered. Theirs is a great love story, but when he dies, the Romans brutalize her and her daughters. In rage, Boudica raises the British tribes and nearly succeeds in driving the Romans from Britain, claiming a place in history. Lhiannon survives and becomes the guardian of the Druid traditions in the new Roman Britannia as high priestess of the Forest House.  Epic in its sweep and peopled by the remarkable women who have always inhabited Avalon, Marion Zimmer Bradley?s Ravens of Avalon expands the legendary saga that has enchanted millions of readers over
